How should your community work? Your customers know. Through your sales group, find your enthusiasts, and ask them how they'd prefer to participate. They will become important leaders in your online community, so keeping their support and enthusiasm is crucial. If you have celebrity customers, for example Olympic stars for a snowboard manufacturer, or home decorators for a furniture company, you can reach out and secure and pay for their participation in advance. Plan to drive traffic to your community. The moment you open the doors to your community, here's what will happen. Nothing. The community knows you exist. Tariq Mahmoud at Linksys uses the company's on hold system and website in a coordinated fashion to drive traffic. You should do the same. Advertise on sites where your customer's shop. Put the community's web address on the cover of the owner's manual. And don't forget search engines. Once your support community or Wiki is buzzing with activity, posts will show up high in search fields. Until then, you'll want to buy paid search listings at Google, Yahoo, and MSN Live Search. Build in a reputation system. Allowing participants to build up a reputation is crucial. Ask Lyle Fong, the CEO and co-founder of Lithium Technologies, the company that built many of the forums you see on company websites, including the forum that saved Christmas for Linksys. According to Lyle, users will spend hours a day building their reputations in a community. A well-built reputation system encourages users to participate and behave in the right manner. The reputation system is what allows Jeff Stensky, with his 20,000 posts on the Dell community forum, to be recognized as a contributor at the Diamond level, which makes his advice more credible. And it's the accumulation of points that helps people like Judas Rabbi, Rise Higher and the Rankings at Yahoo Answers. The community develops game-like mechanics. This way, it isn't just the altruistic impulse, but also the competitive impulse that drives participants. Let your customers lead you. Communities have opinions on everything. They'll not only tell you what product features to add, they'll also tell you how the community should run and what you are doing right and wrong. Be sure to include a thread called Improving this community and pay close attention to what you hear, not just there, but throughout the forum. So take the plunge. Let your customers support each other, but you'd better be prepared for how this will change the way you do business. How Groundswell Support Communities will Change Your Company. A funny thing happens when you participate in community forums. People expect you to listen and respond to them. CBS and the producers of Jericho found this out in the most dramatic way possible as they waited through nut deliveries in their offices. But regardless of the forums or wikis you create and participate in, be clear. You're creating your own little part of the Groundswell. Your forum or wiki will become a laboratory where customers talk about everything. Not just your products and how to use them, but also your pricing, your competitors, your salespeople's tactics, your new product roadmap, your company's stock, everything from your logo to the labor practices in your factories. Look at this as an opportunity. By owning or participating in a community, you get a front row seat. Support departments don't typically gain a lot of influence in corporations. They're a cost center. But because support communities are so much richer in content, they can influence development and marketing. The owner of the community, with all this information in her pocket, gets a seat at the table. Since you'll be communicating back to the people in these environments, you can ask them to clarify the meaning of their comments. This dialogue, especially with your most active customers, inevitably draws them into your development process. You end up collaborating with your customers to create better products. That's taking the power of psychic income and building it into your business.

---

## Groundswell - Expanded and Revised Edition_ Winning in a World Transformed by So - 0010 - Chapter 10

9. Embracing the Ground Swell In a tiny town in central Pennsylvania lives a guy named George. George loves his dog, Pooch. Pooch is a cockapoo, which is a fuzzy cross between a spaniel and a poodle. George and Pooch are very close. We enjoy taking walks and running, says George. We also go hiking and enjoy playing in the yard. I would be lost without my pooch. Gala Amarosa was very interested in people like George. Gala is senior consumer insights manager for Del Monte Foods. She spent her whole career peaking into the psychology of people who buy products and grocery stores. She describes doing research on consumers as detective work. That's what I love to do, she says. Understand what the story is, how to connect the dots, and come up with an insight. With help from the brand monitoring vendor, Umbria, Del Monte had identified a segment of pet owners it calls, dogs are people too. We bet you know somebody like this. Dogs are people too, includes people who treat their pets like members of the family. They take them on trips, they play with them anytime they can, they make those dogs part of their lives from sun up to bed time. And they've got a soft spot for pet treats that fit their lifestyles. That was a fair description of George. So when Del Monte and its community vendor, market tools started up a private community called I Love My Dog, Dogs are people too. George was easy to recruit. And an amazing conversation began. On one side where Gala and the folks from Del Monte trying to figure out what products to make, how to package them, and how to sell them. On the other were George and his fellow pet lovers who are happy to explain in detail with lots of back and forth what they were looking for. Gala, throw a market tools moderator asked, what if your dog was a person? Okay, I know your dog is a person. Imagine that your dog could write out his or her fantasy of what to eat for one day. What would that look like? What would he or she write? Click the comments link below to write out what your dog would want to eat for breakfast on your doggy's ideal perfect day. George responded. For breakfast she would definitely want bacon and eggs and she would want ketchup on her eggs. She loves ketchup. A theme began to emerge from the responses of George and the other 400 people in the community as they described the perfect breakfast for their dogs. Dog lovers went doggy treats that look like people food. One asked for McDonald's breakfast pancakes, eggs, bacon, hash brown with a cappuccino. Another said, breakfast would have to be eggs with lots of cheese and he loves cheese. The dogs had spoken through their owners. They wanted bacon and eggs. So Del Monte decided to give them bacon and eggs. Us was born snossages breakfast bites, a breakfast snack that looks just like little strips of bacon and fried eggs. What should be in snossages breakfast bites? How about vitamins? People take their vitamins in the morning. Why not dogs? Gala and Del Monte to the community. Would you buy more treats if they had vitamins and minerals as ingredients? George, I would buy healthier treats over ones that didn't have vitamins and minerals. I tried to look for amino acids and omega-3 in fish oils. I also take these and know they are essential. It is hard to find a variety of snacks that are healthy, so I look for the healthiest brands. 54% of the community responded that yes, they would definitely buy a snack with vitamins and minerals. Next decision? What to put on the package? Gala posted a picture of the package on the community. In addition to a happy cartoon dog snacking on bacon and egg-shaped treats, the package featured this text. A healthy, great tasting start to your dogs day. Calcium for healthy bones and teeth. Antioxidants to promote a healthy immune system. Omega-3 and 6 fatty acids for healthy skin. Again, George responded. Dogs are people too. They need all the necessary vitamins and minerals to lead a long and healthy life. A lot of dog foods in the past were made of junk and fillers. Now they are getting wise to the fact that owners want better nutritious foods for their animals. And if they want to stay in business, they better deliver. I love seeing what that package says because it shows they care. George was not alone. The comments from the community poured in. Snowsages breakfast spites were ready. They would sell. So, Delmont, he started making them. And it's early yet, but from all appearances, they are selling. Delmont had found a way to make a few dog lovers happy. Product development is hard. Why not let your customers help you with it. Embracing customers. A new kind of development. Innovating with help from your customers is such a great idea that there have already been at least three books written about it. Eric Von Hipples groundbreaking democratizing innovation. Goes into detail about the role of lead users in helping influence development of new products from software to surfboards. Patricia B. Seabulls outside innovation, how your customers will co-design your company's future provides dozens of examples of ways to harness customer innovation in development processes with tools like private communities, ratings and reviews. And Don Tapscott and Anthony D. Williams's WikiNomics, how mass collaboration changes everything, looks at ways to use the collaborative Wikipedia model to tap the global community to create innovations. These books are great, and the vision they demonstrated is spectacular. But when it comes to collaborating with your customers, really bringing them into your development and innovation processes, the one real truth is this. It's challenging. Your company almost certainly grew in ways designed to direct the innovation into manageable places. You probably have a department that's in charge of product development with a lot of smart engineers who have a lot of smart ideas. You probably have a market research department, with people like Gala Amoroso in it that's charged with researching what your customers want. And it's not just products. When it comes to services or internal processes, there are probably people whose job it is to identify ways to improve those services and processes. If you want to open up new distribution channels, you need to work with a channel expert and deal with pushback from the people in charge of the old channels. But the fact is, as you've seen in all the chapters leading up to this one, your customers are chomping at the bit to tell you what to do. They're both complaining and praising your products on forums, their rating and reviewing your products on retail sites, their blogging and making videos and analyzing your every move. Whether you're ready or not, they are now part of your process. They are leaning over your management's shoulders. If you've already undertaken one of the goals in chapter 5 through 8, if you're listening to, talking with, energizing or supporting the ground swell, then you've already opened up a channel of communication with those customers. And inevitably, the insights that come from that, the relationships you're developing with those customers, will lead them deeper into your company. We call this embracing the ground swell, making customers an integral part of the way you innovate, with both products and process improvements. This chapter is about finding practical ways to tap into customers to help you innovate now, to increase the chances of developing something your customers want. And it's not just about better products and processes. It's about innovating faster. Why innovating faster is powerful? The cases in this chapter have one thing in common. They show how, by embracing the ground swell, you can move more quickly. There are two reasons for this. First of all, customers don't take long to tell you what they want. They use your product and interact with your company. They know what's wrong and how things can be fixed. They have ideas your development people and management haven't thought of yet. As soon as you're ready, you can tap into these sources of innovation. People who've connected with customers in this way are always amazed at how much more quickly they generate ideas. It's because they just supercharged their dozens or hundreds of engineers with thousands or millions of other minds. Second, with customers in the loop, innovation happens more quickly because you can iterate, make continuous improvements. Look at the conversation Gala Amoroso had with her customer community. First, she asked what the product should be. Then she asked whether the product should have vitamins. Then she asked about the packaging. That's three decisions, three iterations in a few short weeks. When you're tapped into the customer community, you get feedback quickly. And once you've processed it, you can go back and ask the next question. It says if we replace an exchange of letters with a face-to-face conversation. Everybody knows you get more done face-to-face and that's because conversations are more efficient than asking questions in a survey, conducting an engineering study, or having executives review every suggestion. In this chapter, we'll look at three cases. How salesforce.com uses an innovation community to involve customers in the design of new products. How a French credit union made customer suggestions a part of how it does business. And how a Canadian grocery store uses ratings and reviews to improve its products. In each case, these companies are moving faster than they ever did before, which saves money and makes life difficult for competitors. Case Study salesforce.com Embracing through an innovation community. Speed is important to Steve Fisher. Steve is the VP in charge of the platform. Basically, the foundation of the product in its development environment at salesforce.com. Salesforce.com makes customer relationship management applications. If you're a salesperson, you open up salesforce.com on your PC and use it to manage the opportunities and leads you're working on. Salesforce.com also feels a similar role in service and marketing departments. But salesforce.com itself isn't software. It's an on-demand service delivered through the internet. That means salesforce.com can deliver updated and improved capabilities far more quickly. And that's why speed is important to Steve. Salesforce.com evolves rapidly. The company used to put out three new releases per year. But the process had developed some frustrating snarls. Developers, the tech wizards who build the application, and marketing people often disagree about what made sense to add next. The obvious answer was to see what the customers wanted. The problem wasn't that salesforce.com wasn't listening. It was the Blizzard of Requests. 10,000 customer requests had piled up. Some of those were great ideas. Others weren't. The problem was telling which was which. In 2006, one of the product managers at salesforce.com came across an application called Crispy News and saw what could be a solution to the problem. Crispy News works much like Dig, allowing visitors to vote entries up or down based on what they like. But unlike Dig, Crispy News was an application that any company could license. We were looking at doing this ourselves, said John Tashek. Salesforce.com's VP of Market Strategy. But Crispy had the technology to find out the kinds of things people wanted to know. And what people at salesforce.com wanted to know was which features would be most popular and relevant for customers. In the fall of 2006, salesforce.com launched the salesforce.com idea exchange. Ideas.salesforce.com and invited customers to itemize their development priorities. Before this, customers ideas had fallen like snowflakes, enveloping the development process in an undifferentiated blanket of suggestions. Now the ideas were channeled and directed by the groundswell of salesforce.com's own customers. In one year, over 5,000 ideas arrived. Now the best ones bubbled to the top. The customers were organizing their priorities for salesforce.com. Not all of these were easy for salesforce.com to swallow. One of the first and most popular suggestions addressed the SawBanner, a text added that popped up every time a customer logged into salesforce.com. The SawBanner was beloved in salesforce.com's marketing department, since it enabled the company to communicate directly with everyone using the software about new releases, conferences, and the like. But people attempting to get work done weren't so happy with it. Here's what a customer known as FIFTH Dog posted on the Ideas. Begone SawBanner's, please be gone, followed by a graphic of the SawBanner. I know I'm not alone on this, however, I want to bring this up and see if we can't get some relief from these annoying banners that show up on our screens every time we log into salesforce. If you would like to try and remedy the issue, please vote yes on this idea to stop the insanity. This was rapidly followed by over 6,000 votes and hundreds of impassioned comments in favor of the idea of scrapping the SawBanner's. And that caused a conflict at salesforce.com. On the one side were Steve and many of the developers who wanted to maximize the application's usability. On the other were salesforce.com's marketers who had grown dependent on the SawBanner to connect with their customers. Who won? Thek customers did. On this issue, both sides had compelling arguments. But in the end, salesforce.com was trying to satisfy its customers, which added heft to Steve's side of the discussion. In the 9 months he took to settle this argument, the company had learned that many of the customers' other suggestions were excellent and had begun to integrate those suggestions into its products. So when the time finally came to tackle the SawBanner issue, salesforce.com decided to trust its customers. And as FIFTHDUG had requested, it was begun to the SawBanner. Idea Exchange has revolutionized the way salesforce.com develops products. Steve, who likes speed, got what he liked. In 2007, salesforce.com pumped out four new releases in contrast to only two in 2006. New releases now include 300 new features, three times as many as in previous years. Why did things change? Confidence. Half of the new features now come from suggestions in Idea Exchange. Instead of holding big meetings to wrangle over features, developers can move forward knowing what people want. This makes for less wasted effort and more progress. We can help diminish the political pushing and make it about the quality of the ideas says Steve Fisher. He describes the new process as real debate about real ideas. You want to surface those and get rid of the crap. The crap, as he puts it, the features that were developed based on personal prejudices and guesswork won't survive true collaboration and social networking. The ads that Idea Exchange gave us back our velocity and for a man who likes speed in a company that moves briskly, that's worth a lot. Idea Exchange's work if you can engage your customers. It's interesting to compare salesforce.com's experience embracing customers with Dels. Dels Ideastorm, www.delideastorm.com uses the same system that Salesforce.com does. Just like Salesforce.com, Del has a high level of participation, 7,000 ideas, and 500,000 votes cast as of this writing. And like Salesforce.com, Del has taken advantage of the ideas coming from the community. As a company that sells to consumers, Del naturally has a harder time connecting with customers in an Idea Exchange. Most of Del's customers aren't interested enough in its products to contribute. In contrast to Salesforce.com, which had convinced nearly 10% of its customers, in businesses, to join Idea Exchange. But even so, Del has been able to get thousands of his customers into Idea Storm enough to make the site a productive source of ideas for the company. One was a PC running Linux, the open source operating system, instead of Windows. With the confidence that came from Idea Storm, Del tapped the community to decide on features, support methods, even the type of Linux to install. As a result, the Linux PC went from Idea to finished product in just two months, versus the 9-15 months that usually takes to ship a new machine at Del. Nobody at Del believes that Idea Storm is a representative collection of its customers. Del knows that Idea Storm is filled with the more technical members of its customer community and treats the suggestions accordingly. Even so, the Linux machine is selling above expectations. Could you create an Idea Exchange at your company? If you have impassioned customers, they'll be happy to join a forum and influence your product development. While this could be a good source of ideas, recognize that it doesn't hit everybody in your audience. The concept works, but it reaches a skewed subset of the customer base. And for many companies, there simply aren't enough enthusiastic customers to support an Idea Exchange. These companies need another simpler way to tap into their customers' desires. They should just ask customers how they can be better, as Kredi Mutuel did. Case study, Kredi Mutuel asking your customers for ideas. Kredi Mutuel is a French regional cooperative bank with 10 million customers. Its slogan is La Banque à Kiparele, the bank you can talk to. Recently, the company was testing an ad campaign on a focus group. Respondents thought the slogan was hollow. What do you mean the bank you can talk to, they asked? How can we talk to you? And if we do talk to you, what happens next? The amazing thing about this story and the reason that Kredi Mutuel lives up to its slogan is this. The bank decided it would listen. It would ask people what they wanted, and it would act on their suggestions. Early in 2007, Kredi Mutuel began its ad campaign. Viewers were directed to a website with the unlikely name, cjtabonkie.com. Cjtabonkie means if I were a banker. The website invites people in with a voice that says, in French, of course. If I were a banker, I would give my customers a say in things, and together we would build the bank of tomorrow. From there, there are two main choices. Enter a suggestion or see other people's suggestions. Once a week, the bank randomly selects one person who made a suggestion to receive an iPod. Now, logic says this shouldn't work. Nobody wakes up and says, if I were a banker in the morning, except maybe people really hoping to get a job as a banker. And the bank took a risk. What if all the suggestions were useless? What if people flooded the site with obscenities? Stealing itself, Kredi Mutuel began to run the ads and designated 10 staff to sort through the suggestions and weed out the useless ones. But I so often happens in the grounds well, people defied expectations. Kredi Mutuel received tens of thousands of suggestions. 15% of them were useless, like if I were a banker, I would change jobs. But the rest were interesting. Some were very interesting. The bank picked the 30 best suggestions. One was this. I would explain the fees better to my customers. Not drop the fees, just explain them. The bank realized that charges on its statements were mystifying its customers. This one's getting fixed already. All branches will be distributing a pamphlet with all the fees listed on it. That's easy. How about this one? Create a fictitious account that children could manage on the internet so the children get used to managing a bank account. With Kredi Mutuel staff have thought that went up. That's apparent talking and it's a pretty interesting idea, completely out of left field. The top ideas are all completely different and all reasonable. Like, I would commit to a deadline to reply to questions sent by email or left at the reception desk. Or, I would discount insurance for young drivers whose parents the bank has been ensuring for years. In September 2007, the bank put all 30 of the top ideas online and let people vote on them. And it's already beginning to implement many of these suggestions. If this sounds a lot like Salesforce.com's idea exchange, it is. But unlike Salesforce.com's customers, the banks aren't likely to spend a lot of time coming back to a site whose only purpose is to evaluate banking ideas. The whole reason the campaign is called, if I were a banker, is that most people don't normally think like a banker. So a site that lets you submit a suggestion quickly and move on makes more sense than a banking idea exchange. There's a lot right with this plan. First of all, the bank has aligned itself on the same site as its customers. Instead of saying, tell us what to do, it's said and the differences subtle, what would you do if you were us? By encouraging the customers to develop empathy for the bank, even momentarily, creating mutual gets much more realistic suggestions. Second, the bank has tapped into a major source of innovation here. Its customers know what has to be improved. All it had to do was ask the right question and be ready to listen and sort through suggestions, of course. Third, the bank is listening. It says it's listening. People are noticing its listening. The bank you can talk to are no longer just words. The next time a French citizen needs to pick a bank, that could make the difference. Finally, the bank has not abdicated its responsibility to develop good services. Any more than Del Monte has forgotten how to make pet products, or Dell has forgotten how to design computers. It's still the bank's responsibility to figure out how to be a great bank. Bank employees can ignore some of those suggestions, but now that they have just a little help from their customers, it may be easier to prioritize ideas for improvement. The cost of the ads is large, but Crady Mutuel would have spent this money on some type of brand advertising regardless. For the incremental cost of 10 staffers and 26 iPods over six months, probably less than $350,000, the bank improves itself and it improves its image. What happens next? Let's assume that Crady Mutuel decides to act on many of these suggestions. Along one path, Crady Mutuel becomes a different organization. After acting on the suggestions, it could publicize what's been done. Having seen that the bank is improving, Crady Mutuel could make listening and acting, embracing its customer suggestions, a regular part of how it does business. CJTA Banquea would become a permanent part of the company's culture, and from that point on, Crady Mutuel would improve itself continuously with its customer's help. In a world where banks are pretty much alike, it would be demonstratively more responsive. Alternatively, Crady Mutuel could stop embracing its customers when the ad campaign ends. The bank you can talk to would go back to being just a slogan, and one that people would stop believing. All the brand equity the bank could build up would ev'o way, and worse, it would cease to tap into its customers' innovations and go back to business as usual. A few words about crowdsourcing. The Crady Mutuel story is an example of crowdsourcing, asking the groundswell to provide you with ideas. Crowdsourcing is all the rage right now. It's especially popular with advertising agencies which are increasingly asking people to create television ads as part of some sort of contest. Frito Lay's Doritos ad in the 2007 Super Bowl was crowdsourced. It was pretty good too. Crowdsourcing by itself is not the same as embracing your customers. Crowdsourcing ad campaign, and you might save a few bucks on ad production, but you won't have to do the hard work of changing the way you interact with customers. Salesforce.com has permanently changed how it innovates. Del Monte thinks very differently about creating new products now, and Crady Mutuel, if it keeps going in the same direction, will be a very responsive bank in the future. On the other hand, Frito Lay probably learned very little from crowdsourcing its Super Bowl Doritos commercial. It's unlikely to turn over significant parts of its ad creation process to consumers on a regular basis. Its customers are not changing the company's product development pipeline, supporting each other, or energizing each other in any sustainable way. Crowdsourced ads are a flash in the pan. They tap the groundswell for a moment rather than move the company in a positive direction. Case study, Loblaw, reviews that drive continuous improvement. Jim Osborne is helping turn a grocery store and its store brand into a hotbed of innovation. Jim is VP of e-commerce and online marketing for Loblaw, a huge Canadian grocery chain, and Canada's largest retailer, with over a thousand stores coast to coast. Loblaw is not just a retailer but also a manufacturer. Its president's choice store brand accounts for more than 20% of all sales throughout the chain. Loblaw creates a thousand new president's choice brand products a year. A central part of the president's choice brand is that Loblaw listens to and responds to its customers. In chapter 7, we showed how ratings and reviews can boost sales on a website. At Loblaw, the focus is also on ratings and reviews, but not for web sales. The company uses ratings and reviews by bizarre voice to close the loop in the store. If you shop at Loblaw, reviews are everywhere. PC rated by you is a trademark of president's choice and the logo is visible throughout the stores. Loblaw drives and supports reviews and the strategy works. Over 300,000 customers are registered on its website. Loblaw even had the guts to ask in every review, do you love this product? And post the answers, both positive and negative comments, right on its site. On the store flyer, the PC vegetable lasagna gets 4.5 stars out of 5. We all enjoyed it. Even my vegetable hating 17 year old son, raves a customer right on the flyer. With all the reviews, Loblaw flyers can look a little more like movie ads than food ads. Walk down the aisle in the store and you'll often see big signs with similar messages, including a customer quote. Just like eBags in chapter 7, Loblaw lets every site visitors see every review. And also like eBags, Loblaw gets about 80% positive reviews. But what sets Loblaw apart is it with its own store brand selling so well it can continuously improve its own products. The Greek Musaka was good, but not good enough. People wanted more eggplant. Loblaw doubled the eggplant and bragged about it in the advertising flyer and on the package. The chicken breast stuffed with mushrooms and provolone wasn't as popular. People complained it leaked when you cooked it. Loblaw fixed it. Making improvements based on customer suggestions is now baked into the process at Loblaw. We'll take the feedback and pipe it right back to the product developers and pat ourselves on the back, says Jim Osborne. The ones that tank they may discontinue, but often they will analyze, reformulate and re-release. Failed products at Loblaw are just an opportunity to try again and fix things. Our favorite story is about PC's Smokin Stampede BBQ Sauce. Loblaw took it to the Ozark Hog BBQ Championship in Batesville, Arkansas, not normally a hotbed of Canadian cuisine. A film crew recorded a local who tasted the sauce, loved it and put it on his brisket, which went on to win the BBQ contest. Loblaw knew it had a great product and brought it back to Canada where it proceeded to get an average rating of 9.4 out of 10, promoted with video from Arkansas. What's not to like? The cap turns out the bottle was too tall for some refrigerator shelves and the spring-loaded cap was driving people crazy. Customer reviews revealed the flaw. Loblaw improved the packaging and fixed the problem. The customers still love the taste and they're no longer complaining about the bottle. For Loblaw, increasing market share for the president's choice store brand increases profits. Improving products boosts that equation. There isn't a PC product that's immune to the benefits of suggested improvements coming in from customers. But there's more to this story than that. Loblaw and president's choice are listening and customers know it. Everyone who shops knows products have problems, but it's the rare retailer or manufacturer that actually fixes them. Loblaw has that reputation now which means a lot to its customers. Can you learn from Loblaw? Are you ready to change your company's reputation? Do you want people to think of you as a company that listens? President's choice in Loblaw have some special advantages over their competitors. A powerful brand and store under the same corporate ownership make this example work. Walmart could do this. It store brands outsell many national brands. So could Sony, LL Bean, Sears, and countless other brands that have their own stores. But they'd have to prove they're ready to listen to their customers and act on those customers' suggestions right at the point of sale like Loblaw. Most stores and brands aren't humble enough to do that. Even brands and manufacturers without their own stores can get feedback. For example, eBags provides reports to its suppliers about what its customers are saying. What about your brand? Are you scrutinizing weekly point of sales data to evaluate how well your promotions are doing? Maybe you'd be better off investing a little of that effort into reading customer ratings and reviews from your retail partners. Then you'd learn why things are selling or aren't. How humility and creativity can coexist whatever business you're in. When it comes to embracing your customers, there are two things to remember. The first is that it doesn't matter what kind of business you're in. If you have customers, they can help you. If you're in a consumer service business like a restaurant chain, you can ask for suggestions like create a mutual or set up a private community like Del Monte. If you sell to businesses as Salesforce.com does, your customers may have suggestions on how to improve your processes, your pricing, your billing, or your services. The key is you need to do more than just ask for feedback. You need to show that feedback online, good and bad, where people can see that you are ready to act on it, that you are committed to improving things. While it's tough to pull all that feedback out for everyone to see, it's worth the risk. And face it as we saw in chapter one, your customers are out there dishing you in the ground swell anyway. So you might as well keep the discussion where you can see it. You'll innovate faster and look responsive, and in the long term, that's going to bring more business your way. The second thing to remember is that embracing your customers takes a balance between skill and humility. Take a company like Apple, which generates a lot of loyalty, but doesn't have a highly visible feedback mechanism. Apple has brilliant engineers and marketers, but will it get every single product right every time? Sooner or later, the hot streak is going to end. We're not suggesting that a company like Apple turn its development over to its customers. That would be a tragic waste of talent. No, the companies that win by embracing their customers incorporate those suggestions into their own development and process strengths. The customers don't tell these companies what to do, they just make suggestions. The difference is these companies are listening to and acting on many of those suggestions. That's what accelerates innovation, starting a conversation with your customers and using your skills to understand and exploit their knowledge. So work on both fronts in your company. Muster up the humility to listen and tap into the skill to take what you've heard and make improvements. That's embracing the grounds well, and it pays by shortening the distance between you and your next successful innovation. Now you've seen how social technologies can contribute to each of the five corporate objectives. Listening, talking, energizing, supporting and embracing. In the next chapter, we'll look at how one new technology, Twitter, can support all five of these objectives.

---

## Groundswell - Expanded and Revised Edition_ Winning in a World Transformed by So - 0011 - Chapter 11

Chapter 10. Tapping the Groundswell with Twitter Fadre Nally is a mom. Her three-year-old son, Evan, is a regular little kid. Like a lot of moms and kids, they like to go to McDonald's. Fadre gets a little break from her hectic schedule, and Evan gets a happy meal in a toy. The toy is important, especially when McDonald's puts superheroes into those happy meals. As Fadre puts it, Evan is a mega superhero fan, and thus we must procure the entire collection. One day in August 2010, though, things went wrong at the mall in Raleigh, North Carolina where Fadre and Evan go to McDonald's. Fadre looked up to see tears streaming down Evan's face. I got a girl toy, he said plaintively, showing her the littlest pet-shop toy from the happy meal. Definitely not a superhero. Fadre blogs and tweets about her experiences, especially as a mom. I tweet a lot of little humorous things throughout the day, she told us. People have come to expect that from me. Because this was one of those every mom can relate moments she tweeted it to her 3,500 followers. Oh, at McDonald's. Do you know what it does to a little boy to find the littlest pet-shop in his happy meal instead of a superhero? Now this is not that big a deal. It wasn't going to spread like the HD DVD incident we described in chapter 1. But it did create an opportunity for McDonald's to make a customer happy. Because just as Fadre suspected, McDonald's listens to Twitter and uses it to talk to customers. So she heard back quickly from Katie Holland, at sign KTY underscore McDee, who monitors and responds to McDonald's tweets from the company's customer satisfaction department using a tool called CoTweet. Here's her response. At all things Fadre meltdown, can you follow slash DM me and I can get you the right superhero? Thanks, Katie from McDonald's. Fadre followed Katie's Twitter account and then sent her direct messages, DMs, including Fadre's address, allowing them to have a more private conversation. Soon after that, Evan and his mom received a handwritten note, a dessert coupon, and the elusive Wolverine figure that Evan was pining for. Naturally Fadre blogged effusively about the service in a post called, Hey McDonald's Unlovenant, including a photo of her son smiling brightly with all his action figures. Fadre's blog, which normally gets around 4,000 visitors a month, received 27 comments on this post. Nearly all were highly favorable for McDonald's. Measuring the ROI of this sort of outreach is challenging, but McDonald's is fighting image issues all the time and knows that it's brand and its role in family's lives depend on maintaining people's trust. Here's how they use Twitter to do that. Dick Wyon, director of social media for McDonald's, heads a team of eight people that together monitor Twitter using co-tweet to respond directly to many of the 500 plus tweets directed at McDonald's every day. These are the ones that start with at McDonald's. They do other jobs too. Twitter isn't anyone's full-time job, but they've always got it covered. They also monitor mentions of the company and its various food items, for example the Big Mac, looking for trends and potential problems. They play defense. There are urban legends to debunk the photo of pink goop that purports to be what chicken nuggets are made of. It's a fake. They tweet to point people to the right place for information about the recall of Shrek souvenir glasses that turned out to have trace amounts of cadmium. The company wanted to get the word out, although the glasses were deemed safe by the US Consumer Product Safety Commission. They also play offense, tweeting out promotions. McDonald's, like many popular brands, has a big Twitter following 75,000 as of this writing. These followers, many of whom are McDonald's fans, amplify the message, retweeting it out to their own followers. During the seasonal rollout of the highly popular McRib sandwich, the company tweeted about where it was available and retweeted customers who described how they're loving it. McDonald's also retweets Twitter posts and photos from people who win its monopoly game, providing a subtle reminder for skeptics that yes, there are actual winners. These activities won't display so ordinary marketing for McDonald's. Television is still the 10 poll that holds up McDonald's marketing effort. The Twitter is in the tent now, and by reaching out to McDonald's fans and turning potential detractors like Fadre into promoters, Twitter spreads those brand messages further than television can do alone and at far lower cost. There's more to Twitter than meets the eye. People who don't use Twitter tend to write it off. Twitter updates are limited to 140 characters. What can you say in 140 characters? In fact, the richness of the Twitter packs into its tiny updates is amazing. It caught on not only for the reasons we originally predicted in chapter 2. It's free and open, connects people and gives them power, but also because it's so simple. Signing up is trivial. Posting an update is a piece of cake. It interfaces nicely with mobile phones, which allows people to update from anywhere. And as a result, it's rapidly becoming a key part of the ground swell, driving, reporting on, and extending activity in everything from blogs to social networks. In fact, Twitter is now at the center of a whole ecosystem of interactions. These are a few elements of that ecosystem. Followers. Unlike Facebook, Twitter allows anyone to follow anyone else, unless the person being followed blocks you, which is rare. This not only allows connections to form quickly, it enables some of the people on Twitter to develop followings in the thousands or hundreds of thousands. Some of these people like Britney Spears, at Britney Spears, get their following from being celebrities already. But others, like Brian Solis, at Brian Solis, have gained a following from tweeting interesting things. People with large Twitter followings typically feel a sense of responsibility to inform or entertain their followers and become influencers. Twitter even has a tradition called Follow Friday, in which every Friday people recommend others to follow. Hashtags and searches. By default, all Twitter updates are public, which makes them searchable. This makes it easy for companies to see who's tweeting about McDonald's, or Harry Potter, and what they're saying, a real-time peek into the minds of the Twitter world. The simplest way to see these searches is at search.twitter.com, which works just like any other search, but in real-time. Hashtags are simply terms designed to mark a tweet as referring to a topic, and are indicated with a pound sign, or hash sign, such as pound Super Bowl, or pound FF for Follow Friday. Search is on hashtags or more precise than other searches, since the person who included the hashtag in his or her tweet intended it to be searchable. Twitter reveals the newest popular search terms in its list of trending topics, which taken together can give you an interesting idea of what's popular right now, whether it's Justin Bieber or Iraqi elections. Mensions and retweets. Twitter provides an easy way to reply, or reference another Twitter user within a tweet, simply use the at symbol along with the user's Twitter handle. For example, including at J. Burnoff in a tweet will direct that tweet to Josh Burnoff. Twitter's interface makes it easy to see who's talking to or about you this way. The Mensions tab. Retweeting is another key feature that makes it simple to pass long messages from others to your followers. Retweets allow the best ideas to spread virally, which, given Twitter's immediacy, often happens amazingly quickly. Links. Many tweets include links to websites. This is what makes such short updates so powerful. You can share an article or anything else on the web along with a note recommending it. McDonald's tweets coupon offers and links to its contests, along with news articles they think their followers will enjoy. Fadress sends links to YouTube videos and her own blog posts. It's also easy to tweet links to photos and utilities like Twitter pick make it a snap to share photos from your PC or mobile phone. These links, videos and photos make Twitter far richer than just text updates. Lists. In 2010, Twitter added the ability to create lists of people you follow and these lists can be shared. So you can easily see the tweets from Robert Scobals list of the most influential people in technology or Michael Zhang's collection of Twitterers who post great photos. Apps and tools. Because Twitter feeds are open, there's a huge collection of tools that tap into it for both individual Twitterers and companies. One of the best tools for Twitterers is tweet deck, a free application for collecting your mentions and searches into columns in a full screen interface, making responding easier. The Twitter company acquired or built mobile tweeting apps for iPhone, iPad, BlackBerry and Android users and their competing apps for these platforms as well as for Windows mobile. Tools like Bitly integrated into tweeting applications create short equivalents for long web addresses to make it easier to fit them into 140 characters. On the corporate side, CoTweet is one of dozens of tools including Cloud, a free site that evaluates the influence of Twitter users. And most of the listing platforms we described in chapter 5, as well as customer service systems like right now, also integrate with Twitter. Fast-nating applications like Flipboard, an iPad app and PaperLea, turn Twitter lists in the links they point to into a curated collection of articles, graphics and surrounding tweets. Twitter users have an outsized level of influence. Our survey show that as of mid-2010, only 7% of online adults in the United States were actively tweeting. Why make such a fuss over such a small group? Because they're among the most influential people out there. As you can see from the social-technographics profile of tweeters, there are three times as likely to be creators, more than twice as likely to be critics, and half again as likely to be joiners compared with typical online consumers. In fact, as we describe in our book Empowered, around 10% of all the influence spread in social networks comes from Twitter, even though it's so new. More than 70% of Twitterers say that they often tell friends about products that interest them, which is far above average. Tweeters make more money, are more optimistic about technology, and are better educated than other people online. But Twitterers may not match up to your prejudices. They aren't quite as young as the people who use other forms of social technology. One-third of them are over 35. These statistics tell you that your company needs to connect on Twitter. Like FADRA, most Twitterers are ready to connect with companies that are interested in them. But before you embrace Twitter, you should ask the same thing you would about any other outreach in the ground swell. That's your objective. Twitter can serve many objectives. In contrast to many other forms of social technology, Twitter is quite versatile. In our McDonald's case, the company is using Twitter both for talking and supporting. In chapters 5 through 9, we told you that mixing objectives was dangerous. But when it comes to Twitter, you need to know that once your company starts to connect, people will expect the company to listen and respond, not just broadcast. You still need a primary objective, you just need to be ready for the expectations of your customers. So what's Twitter good for? A better question is, what do you want to use it for? Here's how to use Twitter for each of the five ground swell objectives. Listening to Twitter. In chapter 4, we told you that all companies should start in the ground swell by listening. When it comes to Twitter, listening is essential. Unless you listen to Twitter, you won't know what you're getting into. For example, as we write this, here are a few things people are saying about the swiffer dusting products from Proctor and Gamble. We've left the first two Twitter IDs off, so as not to make anyone inadvertently famous. At least these owners keep the floors clean as evident by the two swiffer mobs leaning against the wall. My brother paid $40 for swiffer its stamps and just found out they're $10 at Walmart, Pound Sign LMFAO. Puristics. Ditch this whiffer, swap your household sweeper to the non-toxic bona. That last tweet came from a site for green personal care products with 368 followers. P&G needn't be worried yet, but may want to keep an eye on the question of whether people are becoming more concerned about green. It might also want to lock up the at swiffer account on Twitter, which someone else currently controls. Obviously, most brands don't have the staff to watch every comment all the time, but someone should be looking at trends and identifying whether some influential individuals are talking about the brand. We've monitored the term groundsall in the last two years since the book was published and identified quite a few readers with interesting ideas and challenges, and encouraged many others as they pursued their own social strategies. Listening tools, including infogy and radian 6, can help with monitoring tweets and identifying overall sentiment. CommunitySpace, the listening company that we open chapter 4 with, has also found a creative way to use Twitter for listening. Together with Best Buy, it recruited several hundred members into a private research community during the 2008 year and holidays. With CommunitySpace's urging, many of these community members set up private Twitter accounts, accounts that only CommunitySpace could see, and then tweeted their impressions as they did their holiday shopping. Best Buy used the resulting insights to help with messaging strategy and the timing of advertising buys. Talking to Twitter Talking on Twitter is easy. It's also typically boring and useless, unless done well. With both of them setting up a Twitter account and tweeting links to your press releases, you won't get a whole lot of followers. Instead, think about what you can offer that might get picked up and repeated by others. It is possible, but unlikely, that you'll be able to tweet as part of a successful strategy without responding to others. For example, at Dell Outlet, tweets for Dell about refurbished and overstocked Dell computers. The tweets are boring. For example, 15% off any Dell Outlet Vostro laptops plus 3-5-day shipping. Enter code QX3Q Question Mark 3G $9N7MWV at checkout HTTP colon slash slash Dell. But this boring Twitter account now has over 1.5 million followers. Dell has driven over $7 million in sales with it. If you want deals on PCs, this account is worth following. Business author Seth Godin also tweets his daily blog post on at this Seth's blog. He has 55,000 followers and follows zero other people. He never responds here and for that matter doesn't take comments on his very popular blog. Until you get as popular and insightful as Seth, you can't get away with this. More typical are companies like Southwest Airlines at Southwest Air 1 million followers. The tweet deals in news but also respond to others. Southwest has recognized that like McDonald's, its presence will demand responses to service questions, not just tweeting news. On the day we looked, Southwest was tweeting, ready to celebrate 12 days of love. After giving away a $1,000 SWA gift card each day, read the rules, HTTP colon slash slash cut out slash H16ORP, pound sign 12 days of love. But it was also responding to people with questions about its planes, its flights and its promotions. For example, when a Twitterer complained that it had some dirty-ass planes, at Southwest Air responded by asking which flight number in city, indicating that it cared and would fix the problem. One group of companies that can talk to Twitter effectively are media companies. News organizations like the New York Times have multiple Twitter feeds and many of their reporters have their own. For example, Amalie Benjamin, Boston Globe Beat reporter for the Red Sox, uses her updates at At Amalie Benjamin to give a fascinating insider view of what's going on with the team. An extreme example is Bill Simmons at Sports Guy 23, an ESPN reporter who has over a million followers and remains engaged with them by tweeting and responding day and night. One of the most effective media presences on Twitter is at Colonel Tribune, the voice of the Chicago Tribune. His name is a take-off on Colonel McCormick, the Chicago icon who was publisher of the Tribune in the early 20th century. Colonel Tribune tweets not only Tribune newspaper stories but other items of interest to Chicago and he even promises to participate in live events, although somehow he never actually shows up. Partly because Colonel Tribune ended up on Twitter's list of people to follow for a while, he now has over 800,000 followers. Bill AD, VP of Digital Development and Operations at the Tribune, credits the Colonel was showing the newsroom the value of social media and of course the Colonel drives traffic to the newspapers pages. An unlike reporter's personal Twitter accounts, he belongs to the Tribune, not to an individual. Energizing with Twitter Remember that energizing means finding people who like your products or services and amplifying their voices. On Twitter, the key to energizing is listening first. This enables you to find the people you want to energize. Energizing on Twitter means responding to fans and retweeting them, as well as giving them content to retweet to their own followers. For example, consider Greg Block, who's director of media relations and new media for San Diego State University. Like any university, SDSU's key communications task is to reinforce ties between the university, student students and its graduates. On the Twitter handle at SDSU News Team, he retweets comments from high school students who are cheering about being accepted and sports highlights about the college's teams. He identifies student complaints and connects the students with people who can help. He deals with more serious media issues like rumors about a student coming down with the H1N1 flu virus. Greg estimates that social applications take up about half his time, but the net effect is to energize students and especially alumni. Keeping them in touch keeps them in a good mood, which helps make fun raising easier. Duncan Donuts is another organization that's become adept at energizing through Twitter. Most of its tweets are in response to customers. Some of them asking questions. Others just praising Duncan Donuts or his coffee for getting them through the day. Duncan's Twitter team assigns tweets for response, often with a link to one of Duncan's programs or his shareable elements on his Facebook page. As Duncan's interactive marketing manager Ben Smith says, we have been able to prove out that Twitter and Facebook are a top of funnel source for acquiring members into our Duncan Perks database, their loyalty program. Duncan also looks for unique opportunities, special fans who can promote its brand. For example, in 2009, when JetBlue announced its all-you-can-jet pass for $600, a couple of guys from Buffalo named Clark Deaver and Joe DeNardo decided to travel to as many of JetBlue's cities as possible, doing 12 hours in a city. According to Ben, Duncan reached out to them on Twitter because the company felt they typified the spirit of the company's tagline, America runs on Duncan. They sent the pair Duncan Donuts cards for use in all those cities, and when they came to the Boston area where Duncan is headquartered, the company representatives met them and hosted them in the corporate box for a Red Sox game. That got Clark and Joe and Duncan featured on ABC News. When it comes to energizing, though, one company stands out. Zappos, the online shoe retailer that is now a division of Amazon, encourages all its employees to use Twitter. Since Zappos has built its reputation on an over-the-top level of service, listening and responding on Twitter make natural sense. And the company would most likely agree that it uses Twitter mostly for a supporting objective, rather than an energizing objective. And highlighting the support activities also an objective, which is why Zappos collects all the Twitter mentions of Zappos, and posts them publicly on its website at http.colonslash-twitter.zappos.com, along with a list of all tweeting employees and all of their tweets. This demonstrates an incredible amount of trust in both its employees and its customers, but it also makes clear to anyone considering buying from Zappos that the company's commitment to service is real as reflected in the groundswell. Zappos CEO Tony Shey tweeting at Add Zappos has gathered 1.7 million followers by behaving just as he expects his employees to behave. Supporting with Twitter, we've already seen that people who use Twitter are influential. As a result, more and more companies are using Twitter to do support. If you create a Twitter account for your company, people will expect you to respond when they tweet their questions and problems to you. And even if you're not there, that certainly doesn't stop people from speaking about you on Twitter. AT&T's management noticed that the company was a Twitter trending topic. A topic highlighted by Twitter for its popularity, from time to time, and not for reasons the company preferred. Many of the tweets were about wireless service problems and its tie-up with the Apple iPhone. But many of those service problems could be fixed. According to Susan Bean, one AT&T's corporate communications staff, much of the Twitter activity was typical customer care, phone broken, problem with the bill, and the people with an actionable problem were the maddest. So AT&T set out to make Twitter into a service channel. They started with 4 people covering both Twitter and Facebook. That number is now up to 21 people working 7am to 10pm every weekday, as well as part of the day on Saturday. They work closely with the PR team to sort out service issues from corporate issues. In addition to the main accounts, AT&T and AT&T customer care, the service people maintain individual accounts with names like AT&T underscore Susan, and AT&T underscore Trevor, and serve around 2000 people every month, both people tweeting directly to AT&T's Twitter accounts, and people whom AT&T identifies by using Twitter search and monitoring. In addition to creating happy customers, AT&T has now blunted threats to its reputation. People with fixable problems have had them fixed. People complaining about the coverage, tweets like the network sucks in Denver, get links to updates on how AT&T is improving its network locally, which changes the conversation from emotional to rational. One customer started tweeting from the handle at AT&T bad service, the company solved this problem and handed the Twitter handle back to AT&T. Even the actor, Aston Kutcher, at AplusK got a quick response to problems he was having, and he used the 4th most popular person on Twitter with 6 million followers just behind Lady Gaga, Britney Spears, and Justin Bieber. AT&T no longer appears on the list of trending topics, in part because they now handle many of the issues and rumors before they start to spread. AT&T's experience is now spreading through the corporate world for any company that does customer service. It's not just Zappos. Comcast has a team of people responding to complaints and questions from ad Comcast cares. Comcast Buy has a system that allows 2500 of its customer service and sales staff to respond to Twitter requests as we describe in empowered. Even PEMCO, an insurer in the state of Washington, listens for unhappy customers and reaches out to them. People may start out with a bit of a criticism, but can be turned into advocates, says Rod Brooks, PEMCO's chief marketing officer. And as the next case study in the chapter will show, it even works for tax software, where Intuit has turned its Twitter support into a competitive advantage. Embracing with Twitter We saw in the previous chapter that embracing the ground swell, collaborating with your own customers on products or marketing strategies, is the most challenging of ground swell objectives. It's even harder to do through Twitter, since you must gather or following and then communicate with them 140 characters at a time. Even so, we've seen companies find ways to embrace their tweeting customers. One quick way to do this is to use Twitter to drive people to surveys as Adobe does. Providing incentives such as sweepstakes drawings can help such tweets to spread. You can also use Twitter to solicit feedback on products. H&R Block does this, for example. Block's tweets from at MyStarBox idea to promote and drive people into its full idea community. But perhaps the best way to embrace with Twitter is simply to engage in dialogue. A company or individuals who develop a following can embrace their ideas. In creating this chapter and throughout our second book empowered, we solicited ideas from the Twitter community for the best examples in case studies. As long as you believe that sources of insight in the ground swell might be useful to you, it's worth it to ask questions on Twitter and see what comes back. Among all the companies we've interviewed for this book, one of the most sophisticated is Intuit. Because of its breadth of innovation, we recognized it with the Forrester Grantsville Award for Company Transformation in 2008. So when a new tool like Twitter came along, it was no surprise to see Intuit embracing it. What was surprising though was how Intuit's use of Twitter for support turned into a powerful driver for word of mouth. K-Study Intuit uses Twitter to help with a taxing experience. It's hard to imagine anything more frustrating for an American than doing your taxes. There are the incomprehensible forms and the financial terminology. There's math, which nearly everybody hates. It's a chore that ends up with a government taking your money. And if you forget some deductions, perhaps more of your money than they deserve. It has that inflexible, looming deadline. And to top it off, if you screw up, you could go to jail. Pain makes people tweet. So if you're one of the biggest companies helping people with their taxes, you pretty much got to be on Twitter. What's notable about Intuit's TurboTax Twitter team though is just how broad that effort had to be and how it ended up paying off. Intuit's embrace of Twitter began with Chelsea Marty, a PR and social media manager at Intuit. She noticed that there tended to be three types of tweets from people doing their taxes. First, there were people who needed tax advice. If I'm single and I just bought a house, what tax forms do I need? Second, there were people who needed advice on how to use software. How do I install this on my Macintosh? And third, there were people commenting on the experience. Notably tweets like, yay, I finished my taxes. All three were opportunities for TurboTax to demonstrate value in bringing their customers closer. So Chelsea began to recruit a team that eventually grew to include 40 people who volunteered to spend at least part of their time responding to Twitter. They included people like Lee Ferris, a CPA with 30 years experience helping with tax issues, and David Cantor, a customer care employee. The team included people from training and software engineering too. The key to the success of this team is timely response. People working on their taxes need help now. The April 15th deadline is inflexible, so in March and April there's a ramp up not only of the use of TurboTax software, but in the urgency with which people need answers. The company naturally pivots to a customer care focus during those months, and that's why employees from departments throughout the company become customer care tweeters during tax time. These staffers respond quickly. In 2010 their average response time was 4 minutes, and they responded to nearly 2,000 tax tweets. There are stream of tweets, visible at at-team TurboTax, is filled with offers to help and links to online information, and those tweets make a difference. In a survey, 48% of them said the advice was effective in helping them to complete their tax returns, and 71% said they would recommend TurboTax to others. Given the high level of social connectedness of Twitter users, those recommendations will carry a lot of influence. Those tweets aren't just creating good service. They're supercharging TurboTax's word of mouth right in the middle of America's tax preparation frenzy. Twitter Lessons It seems as if Twitter logos, whether the blue lowercase T or the bird silhouette, are everywhere now. Setting up a Twitter account is simple, and useless on its own. As we've described throughout this book, Twitter will be effective only if you choose a clear objective and develop a strategy to make progress toward that objective. But because Twitter makes a company instantly accessible, all Twitter strategies need to account for what happens once a company sets up camp. People arrive and bring expectations with them. Twitter is in many ways the visible face of the company in the ground swell, so while this advice applies to all Twitter strategies, it's also good advice for your social strategy in general. Lock up your handle. If you don't tweet from that handle, you'll look bad, but if you let somebody else get your handle, you'll look worse. We've seen brands like Exxon Mobile have to deal with imposter's who snapped up their Twitter handles, and we're pretending to tweet on the company's behalf. Twitter has a verified account program that prominent brands and individuals can use to ensure that people know those handles belong to the real company. Listen first. Know what people are tweeting about you before you start posting. Be ready to support people. Customers will expect your Twitter handle to support them, especially angry customers. They don't care what your objective is. You need a procedure to identify Twitterers who need help and hand them off to your customer service or technical support group. Follow others. Following users costs you nothing. There's no requirement that you pay attention to all their tweets, but it may give them a thrill to say, don't can don't follow me or carnival cruises follow me. Twitter's rules also require that you follow people if you want them to send you private direct messages, a best practice for providing support where people need to share personal information with you. Be ready for a crisis. When something goes wrong, say your oil well in the Gulf of Mexico blows out, where the gas pedals in your car starts sticking to the floor, people will look to your Twitter accounts to respond. That's true, even if you set up the account to help with hiring or tweet out deals. You need a plan to allow your PR people to turn the Twitter handle into an information channel. Those tweets can link to official statements, but people will be expecting them regardless of how you use the account. As you can see from the worst case scenarios we just cited, regardless of who owns your Twitter handle, PR and customer service need to be involved in the way you run it. Respond, retweet, and link. Other handles that broadcast but never respond may be simple to run, but they frustrate your fans and customers. An effective Twitter campaign normally includes all the elements of the rich Twitter experience, responses to people tweeting to you, retweets of tweets or mentions that your followers will find interesting, links to interesting material on your site, news articles, blog posts or anywhere on the web, and links to photos and videos. And a little personality doesn't hurt either. Remember that it's easy for anyone to see your whole tweet stream, so avoid sending the same response over and over again. Staff it. Twitter needs to be in people's job description, even if it's not necessarily their full-time job. Until it's staff of 40 may be way more than you need, you might be more like SDSU with its single part-time Twitter, but Twitter needs to be somebody's job. One of these are marketers, PR people, or support people. Their job description should allow time and their day for tweeting. Check with legal and regulatory staff. Twitter is public speech. Any statements that would be inappropriate to publish, for example, early peaks of financial results, customer data, or advertising statements about regulated products like pharmaceuticals, are inappropriate on Twitter. Remember, Twitter is public, anyone can link to any tweet, and it's searchable. Having gathered a following, don't waste it. Marketers like to use Twitter and ad campaigns, some promotions garner large followings. For example, to build interest in a television program on Air Force One, the National Geographic Channel created an Air Force One Twitter account and promoted it, generating 2,000 followers in less than 3 weeks. But once the program was over, the company had to persuade those people to transition to its National Geographic account. If you're building interest in an account, create a plan for what to do once you're done with it. Twitter may well be among the simplest, most powerful social tools you can use. Just make sure you integrate it with the rest of your social channels. You've reached the end of the strategy section of this book. But as you've seen in all these chapters, once you introduce the ground saw into your company, things change. It's irreversible. In the next four chapters, we'll reveal how those changes happen and what the future will bring.

## Groundswell - Expanded and Revised Edition_ Winning in a World Transformed by So - 0012 - Chapter 12

Part 3. The Ground Swell Transforms Chapter 11. How Connecting with the Ground Swell Transforms Your Company. Rob Master knows how to market when it matters. Rob is US Marketing Director for Skincare Products for the Brand Colossus, Unilever. When he proposed to his girlfriend, he started by creating an elaborate trail of rose petals that led her across Manhattan to the site of their first date. There, Rob popped the biggest question of his life. She always teased me that I wasn't such a romantic guy, Rob said. But I'm a marketing guy. I recognized the importance of how to position things. Rob's ability to position products and ideas turned out to be central to the transformation in how Unilever's Dove Brand approached marketing. Rob, working together with Unilever's US Media Director, Babs Rangaya, helped Unilever not only to accept the internet as a marketing vehicle, but to gradually give up some control of the brand, embracing the power of the ground swell and energizing consumers to make their own contributions. The culmination of that cultural change came when Dove's evolution video debuted on YouTube in the fall of 2006. While Rob and Babs didn't create the video, it came from Dove's agency in Canada, Ogleby Canada, they did help create an environment in which new approaches were welcomed and marketers could take risks. That culture paid off when evolution became a viral success. The 75-second video, which shows a time-lapse view of how an average looking woman is transformed into the epitome of airbrushed, fashion, beauty, was distributed primarily on sites like YouTube, over 5 million people saw it in less than a year. It also garnered advertising's highest awards at the advertising festival in Cannes. Best of all, it caused a surge of traffic to Dove's campaign for real-beauty website, more than double what Dove's 2006 Super Bowl ad drove. The cost of airing a 30-second ad on the Super Bowl, two and a half billion dollars, and the cost of distributing the evolution video on YouTube, Zippo. Let's put this idea into context. In 2006, Unilever spent $2.1 billion on marketing in the United States, with 40% of that spent on media like TV, print, and the web. Moreover, traditional media spending, even when its online media, has been a highly centralized and controlled process. A YouTube video is really about letting go of control and trusting that your creativity will engage the ground swell. So how did one of the largest advertising spenders come to embrace low cost, low control social technologies, which in many ways are the antithesis of mass marketing? It didn't happen overnight. How the ground swell spreads with a customer-centric organization. This chapter will look at two case studies, Unilever and Dell, where big companies transformed a traditional marketing and customer support organization into one that is led by the ground swell. It sounds so logical, so easy. Keep the customer at the center of your organization. But the reality of departmental priorities and ingrained marketing and customer service practices keeps us far from this ideal. Think about it. How many companies have customer managers instead of product managers? One of the benefits we've seen of engaging with the ground swell is that your organization goes through a mental shift. You become so engaged with your customers that you walk in step with their needs and once. The key to this transformation is taking an idea that typically starts with a few employees who understand the ground swell and making it an organization-wide movement. In essence, you want to create your own mini-ground swell within the company to embrace the ground swell of customers outside it. There are three essential elements to this transformation. First, it's important to take this step by step. A mental shift takes time and practice and requires building a repertoire of shared successes. Building these stepping stones is also essential to giving people in your organization an opportunity to adjust their concepts of how things should work. Like a diet plan built on quick weight loss, quick hits and wins can rapidly evaporate if ground swell thinking doesn't become a regular part of a company's processes. We're trying to do all the strategies we outlined in the previous chapters at the same time would be foolhardy. Second, each of these stepping stones leads in a natural progression to the next step. Supporting strategies can lead to talking, which can lead to embracing. You'll need a plan and vision about where you want to take your organization, building a firm foundation from which you can push it up to the next challenging level of ground swell thinking. Third, you have to have executive support. Realistically, you may have to cobble together skunkworks projects to get something off the ground, but you better have in mind how you're going to sell someone in upper management on ground swell thinking if you want your efforts and ideas to catch fire within the organization. In this chapter, we'll look at how organizations can master these three key elements of ground swell thinking to transform how their companies work with customers. Then in chapter 12, we'll look at how companies can do this with their own employees as members of the internal enterprise ground swell. Case study, Unilever, letting go of control to win over the ground swell. As the parent company of brands like Axe, Lipton, and Vaseline, Unilever is synonymous with brand marketing, where grand messages and media plans are meticulously planned, coordinated, and executed over several months. This requires a command and control structure where everything is planned and monitored centrally, especially when several internal and external teams need to be coordinated. One of Unilever's key brands is Dove. In the company and its agency Ogilvian Mayther launched Doves campaign for real beauty in 2004, they took a big risk with a message, going against the industry norm of using young, slender, perfect models, and instead featuring everyday average women. But their radical message was well researched, and it was delivered through traditional channels like any other brand campaign, with a mix of TV, print, and outdoor ads. And it worked. People were definitely talking about the ad campaign and Dove. Next step, learn to give up control of the brand message. Here's where Babs Rangaya enters the story. Babs and Rob conceived the idea of working with the NBC reality television show The Apprentice in an episode where teams created ads for a new Dove product. It took an external opportunity like The Apprentice to create a need for real innovation Babs recalled. There was always some nervousness about how it will turn out. The Dove ads created by the teams on the Apprentice were pretty bad. It wasn't the best way to showcase and launch a product. But the Dove team took advantage of the Apprentice miscuse and the buzz around them to launch their own version of the ad introduced by Apprentice's host Donald Trump himself, which generated a flood of visits to Dove.com. In the end, The Apprentice gamble and the subsequent ad paid off. Babs told us that Dove cool moisture beauty body wash had strong sales results after the episode aired. By 2005, Rob was eager to push the envelope again, and this time the web was central to this plan. Rather than trying to capture a person's attention with 30 seconds of video between sitcom segments, Rob wanted to engage with consumers who wanted to hear from Dove. His proposal created a set of web-based videos to launch a new product line, Dove's calming night, designed to soothe women at the end of a hectic day. He lined up great talent with Penny Marshall directing Desperate Housewives star Felicity Huffman. His plan also stacked the decks for DoveNight.com to succeed by supporting it with full-fledged television, print, and online portal campaigns to drive awareness and word of mouth about the brand and the site. And he built in measurable results by offering free trials on the site. With 3 million visits to DoveNight.com, the company was able to show it could put digital media at the center of an ad campaign. This was a turning point in Unilever's recognition of the power of online. All these experiences had to proceed the evolution video, preparing Dove's management for more and more innovative messages delivered in new ways. Back in 2003, something like the evolution video on YouTube would have been shot down. Instead, Rob, Babs, and innovators from other teams encouraged the cultural change. Babs mentions his boss, Philippe Haruso, and acts Marketers Kevin George and David Rubin, for example. These leaders built Unilever's confidence in the groundswell bit by bit, each time loosening the control reins a little more. It took a lot for us to do that program, a lot of pushing for me and Rob. Once we got it through management, it was the results of the business that allowed us to push that success into other things. Needless to say, it was big and got people at Unilever thinking that, wow, this is a different perspective and can have a major impact on how we can market our brands. We told you that in addition to talking to the groundswell in steps, managers need to build executive support. As the director in charge of coordinating media across all Unilever brands, Babs Ryan Gaia provided the executive support to spread ideas about how to incorporate digital technologies into media plans.
